  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_94fbdec6-1aa8-458a-9dbb-3c71b088b675'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer2594510309373843695">In Monterey, on the California coast, Sweet Thursday is what they call the day after Lousy Wednesday, which is one of those days that are just naturally bad.   <span id="freeText2594510309373843695" style="display:none">In Monterey, on the California coast, Sweet Thursday is what they call the day after Lousy Wednesday, which is one of those days that are just naturally bad. Returning to the scene of <i>Cannery Row</i>, the weedy lots and junk heaps and flophouses of Monterey, John Steinbeck once more brings to life the denizens of a netherworld of laughter and tears from Fauna, new headmistress of the local brothel, to Hazel, a bum whose mother must have wanted a daughter.<br /><br /></span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1849346564" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1849346564" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1849346564?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140048896" href="/book/show/1251385.Sweet_Thursday">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=so-good-it-hurts">so-good-it-hurts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=nobels">nobels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=unforgettable">unforgettable</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1849346564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17609104630001392022">Dear Mack and the boys in Palace Flophouse! <br/> <br/>I can’t tell you how grateful I am for the advice you gave the author in the prologue. You were entirely right, and helped him write a masterpiece! After all, who would know better than you how to describe the intricate balance of talk, action and hooptedoodle, and chapters and settings and characters that form the unforgettable microcosm of Cannery Row? <br/><br/>Uninformed people might get the notion that Cannery Row is a hopeless dump filled with uneducated </span>
  <span id="freeText17609104630001392022" style="display:none">Dear Mack and the boys in Palace Flophouse! <br /> <br />I can’t tell you how grateful I am for the advice you gave the author in the prologue. You were entirely right, and helped him write a masterpiece! After all, who would know better than you how to describe the intricate balance of talk, action and hooptedoodle, and chapters and settings and characters that form the unforgettable microcosm of Cannery Row? <br /><br />Uninformed people might get the notion that Cannery Row is a hopeless dump filled with uneducated tricksters, bums, whores and criminals. But that is not true at all. Cannery Row is a place where people care for each other, help out, throw parties, try to cheer each other up when they are depressed. They fight for their right to their share of love, peace and happiness, and of course they suffer. For they are humans. First-rate feeling, acting, living, cheating, drinking, meddling humans.<br /><br />Starting with good intentions and ambitions, they screw up most of their projects, and ruin themselves and each other in the process, but they NEVER give up caring for Cannery Row and its inhabitants, and trying to do things better next time despite repeated disastrous results.<br />Sometimes that means throwing a masquerade party to raffle off the Palace Flophouse, and a collective effort to get Doc married. Sometimes it means breaking an arm as an act of compassion, and sometimes it means faking a fake horoscope to relieve a poor hero’s anxiety over the burden that he’s destined to become the President of The United States Of America.<br /><br />If Doc and Suzy had not ended up with each other against all odds, they would have broken the hearts of Joseph and Mary, Fauna, Mack and the boys, and especially Hazel, who had to rise to unbelievable heights of thinking to solve Doc’s problem. <br /><br />Whoever can follow the scene where Mack emergency-teaches Suzy how to drive a car with chalk drawn on the floor without laughing and crying, has not got the Cannery Row spirit. Whoever manages not to burst out in bitter laughter when Mack and the boys hand over the gift for Doc, the biggest TELESCOPE in the catalogue, has no heart for the creation of our father who art in nature, as quoted in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4799.Cannery_Row" title="Cannery Row by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">Cannery Row</a>, the prequel to Sweet Thursday:<br /><br />“Our Father who art in nature, who has given the gift of survival to the coyote, the common brown rat, the English sparrow, the house fly and the moth, must have a great and overwhelming love for no-goods and blots-on-the-town and bums, and Mack and the boys. Virtues and graces and laziness and zest. Our Father who art in nature.”<br /><br />What does it matter that it was a MICROSCOPE Doc needed? After all, Steinbeck manages to link the macro cosmos - as seen in a telescope - to the miniature world of Monterey by zooming in the cosmic microscope on the tiny, dusty street of Cannery Row. He re-creates the nature of the whole world in a bitter-sweet, hilarious tale of heroic bums and loving hookers.<br /><br />This is Steinbeck at his best! From first to last page, I enjoyed every toss and turn of the story. Steinbeck can make a literary home of anything, just like Suzy can create a room for herself in a boiler!<br /><br />And it is with bittersweet melancholy that I join Mack in his final, caring statement, closing the novel with a compliment for Hazel, the unthinking bum with the biggest heart of all:<br /><br />“I think you’d of made a hell of a president,” he said.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17609104630001392022" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2189125055" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2189125055" />
    <a title="Brina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2933855-brina"><img alt="Brina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601675085p2/2933855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2189125055?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brina" href="/user/show/2933855-brina">Brina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/34706269-sweet-thursday">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=nobel-prize-winner">nobel-prize-winner</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2189125055" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12718932149692636536">Over the course of this year I have discovered an appreciation for American master story tellers. I have noted in reviews that some of the books assigned to high school children may not be age appropriate and that one gets a deeper appreciation for some of these books by reading them through adult eyes. It is in this regard that I have read Capote, Hemingway, and Steinbeck during this calendar year. A month ago, I found myself enjoying Steinbeck's Cannery Row, only to discover in some comments t</span>
  <span id="freeText12718932149692636536" style="display:none">Over the course of this year I have discovered an appreciation for American master story tellers. I have noted in reviews that some of the books assigned to high school children may not be age appropriate and that one gets a deeper appreciation for some of these books by reading them through adult eyes. It is in this regard that I have read Capote, Hemingway, and Steinbeck during this calendar year. A month ago, I found myself enjoying Steinbeck's Cannery Row, only to discover in some comments to the review that he had written a sequel to this classic. My interest piqued, I made it a point to read this sequel before the end of the year, and once again Steinbeck did not disappoint.<br /><br />It is after World War II. Monterey and the entire state of California is still a sleepy place to call home, although not as behind the times as it was during the interwar years. The faces have changed along the row, but the prime locales remain the same including Chong's Grocery, the Bear Flag, and Western Scientific Institution. Yet, Doc, who had done early leg work toward establishing the Monterey Bay Aquarium during the first book, was one of the protagonists who fought in the World War II. He came home a depressed, changed man, and it became the duty of Mack and his group of vagrant boys from the Flophouse to get the beloved Doc out of his funk. Steinbeck has the entire book, no matter whose story is being told, narrated through Mack's eyes as he attempts to bring Cannery Row back to its prewar form and the once thought of aquarium to fruition.<br /><br />Mack had always been full of ideas and his chief idea here was to get Doc married. He truly believed that a man as smart and noble as Doc needed a girl, preferably a wife, to get him out of his funk. Almost immediately, the entire Cannery Row gets behind Mack and his scheming because everyone loves Doc and do not want to see him fall to rot. Enter the new proprietress of the Bear Flag, Fauna who takes it upon herself to fix Doc up with her best girl, Suzy. Yet, Suzy is no hooker and in her Steinbeck develops a character who is ahead of her time in that she desires independence and is hardened to the point of not needing a man to help her get ahead in life. Not coincidentally, Doc harbors similar thoughts in that his heart has always been with the ocean and its creatures and he has never needed a woman in his life. Yet, Mack is determined and Steinbeck takes this crazy idea of Mack's and develops over the course of the novel.<br /><br />Steinbeck's story telling skills are impeccable. Normally I am apprehensive to read sequels because I am already familiar with the characters and I feel that their story has for the most part already been told. I actually found Sweet Thursday, named for the day in which Mack hopes his dreams for Doc comes true, to be better than Cannery Row. In the first book Steinbeck takes his time introducing his readers to the time, place, and characters. Even with his storytelling ability, the flow to the novel felt choppy. Yet, in the second book, he comes out and announces that his goal is to get Doc married off if it is the last thing he does. As a result, the novel moved faster than its predecessor and I found myself truly enjoying the story, rooting for Doc and Suzy despite their shortcomings, to see if they did indeed find a way to love in the end.<br /><br />Reading Steinbeck through adult eyes has allowed me to enjoy his writing in a more mature light. I hope to read more of his novels in the coming year as he was truly gifted with superior storytelling skills. I would have liked to see the Monterey Bay Aquarium actually open during the course of this book, but that is best left for one's imagination, besides which it may not have existed during the course of Steinbeck's life. Regardless, the Nobel Prize is one of the United States unrivaled story tellers and I am happy to have rediscovered his work this year.<br /><br />4.5 stars (downgraded because I really was hoping to see the aquarium open)</span>
  <a data-text-id="12718932149692636536" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1214963322" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1214963322" />
    <a title="Dolors" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors"><img alt="Dolors" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502350013p2/18383778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1214963322?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolors" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolors" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors">Dolors</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141185521" href="/book/show/609516.Sweet_Thursday">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Good-hearted optimists</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Dolors by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/5431458-henry-avila">Henry Avila</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-in-2016">read-in-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1214963322" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer65130294888362669">The War is over and life seems to go back to normal in Cannery Row. Mack and the boys return to their feisty drinking at the <i>Palace Flophouse</i> and Fauna and the girls recover their usual business at the <i>Bear Flag</i>. Companionship and good cheer, camaraderie and the right dose of picaresque reign over the marginalized neighborhood in Monterey, and the familiar pulse of tragicomedy takes hold of the comforted reader.<br/><br/>There is only a discordant note in Steinbeck’s symphony that dulls the otherwise colo</span>
  <span id="freeText65130294888362669" style="display:none">The War is over and life seems to go back to normal in Cannery Row. Mack and the boys return to their feisty drinking at the <i>Palace Flophouse</i> and Fauna and the girls recover their usual business at the <i>Bear Flag</i>. Companionship and good cheer, camaraderie and the right dose of picaresque reign over the marginalized neighborhood in Monterey, and the familiar pulse of tragicomedy takes hold of the comforted reader.<br /><br />There is only a discordant note in Steinbeck’s symphony that dulls the otherwise colorful picture: old Doc has come back home from the front a changed man; his boundless love for the sea, its marine creatures and even the appeasing voices of Gregorian chants have lost their allure. Doc has run out of the selfless love that kept all the community tied up together and an impending sense of dread has darkened the mood of the whole neighborhood.<br />Trying to reconnect with his former self and screaming out in stifling loneliness, Doc reopens his laboratory and embarks on a new project to study the behavioral pattern of octopuses in situations of stress (yes, Steinbeck’s humor shines with gusto), thinking that hard work will raise his spirits while ignoring the inner voice that nags mercilessly at him about what is missing in his life. <br />Luckily for Doc, Mack and Fauna know exactly what he needs to be a complete man again, and an improbable sequence of events will entertain and warm the heart of any reader who was missing the gang of crooks, vagrants, prostitutes and drunks that live in peaceful fellowship in Cannery Row.<br /><br />Once more, Steinbeck’s prose is a delight to read. With loads of self-effacing and good-natured humor, he uses comedy and rich vocabulary salted with fine irony and a pinch of surrealism to create characters that will beat the odds and prevail over the grim circumstances that have been imposed on them.<br />Forty chapters with titles, sporadic poetry and plenty of philosophy fill the pages of this endearing tale of love and friendship. Time takes the best of us, and new yearnings plague even the most lone wolves when the moment is right. <br /><i>The sea is lonely without its fish.</i> Doc’s solitude is the birth of a metamorphosis that will bring some anguish and self-doubt but also the only chance that his dreams and wishes will materialize and make him whole. If you had such an opportunity, would you let it pass by without risking everything you have to get it?<br />I thought so, and so did Steinbeck.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="65130294888362669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1935248557" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1935248557" />
    <a title="Lawyer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3025547-lawyer"><img alt="Lawyer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603672493p2/3025547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1935248557?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lawyer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lawyer" href="/user/show/3025547-lawyer">Lawyer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=john-steinbeck">john-steinbeck</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=monterey-california">monterey-california</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=friendship">friendship</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=community">community</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=cannery-row">cannery-row</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1935248557" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16083166904272531427"><b>Sweet Thursday: Cannery Row Redux</b><br/><br/><blockquote>The world is so full of a number of things,<br/> I'm sure we should all be as happy as kings.-<i>Happy Thought, from a Child's Garden of Verse, <b>Robert Louis Steverson</b></i></blockquote><br/><br/>If you love the works of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/585.John_Steinbeck' title='John Steinbeck' rel='nofollow noopener'>John Steinbeck</a> as I do, it does not matter whether the critics have labeled one of his novels among his major novels. Even a "minor" Steinbeck is a joy to pick up and read. Even re-read. Recently I reveled in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4799.Cannery_Row' title='Cannery Row by John Steinbeck' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cannery Row</a>. Originally published in 1947, Steinbeck set the novel in the w</span>
  <span id="freeText16083166904272531427" style="display:none"><b>Sweet Thursday: Cannery Row Redux</b><br /><br /><blockquote>The world is so full of a number of things,<br /> I'm sure we should all be as happy as kings.-<i>Happy Thought, from a Child's Garden of Verse, <b>Robert Louis Steverson</b></i></blockquote><br /><br />If you love the works of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/585.John_Steinbeck" title="John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">John Steinbeck</a> as I do, it does not matter whether the critics have labeled one of his novels among his major novels. Even a "minor" Steinbeck is a joy to pick up and read. Even re-read. Recently I reveled in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4799.Cannery_Row" title="Cannery Row by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">Cannery Row</a>. Originally published in 1947, Steinbeck set the novel in the waning years of the Great Depression. It was natural to jump into <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/56077.Sweet_Thursday__Cannery_Row___2_" title="Sweet Thursday (Cannery Row, #2) by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">Sweet Thursday</a>.<br /><br />Steinbeck's return to the Row was published in 1954. Although the setting is the same the community has gone through significant changes. Time has jumped forward to the years following World War Two. The Sardine canneries that flourished, providing the main economic flow in Monterey have closed. The fisheries have been over harvested during the war years.<br /><br />Familiar faces are absent. Lee Chong has sold his store and sailed to the South Pacific. Dora, the madam of the Bear Flag has died in her sleep. Gay, one of the boys living at the Palace Flophouse and Grill was killed in the war.<br /><br />New faces have arrived on the Row in their places. Lee sold his store to Joseph and Mary, known as the Patron, a con man and chiseler, looking to exploit the growing immigrant population. Flora, Dora's older sister has taken over the Bear Flag with a new perspective on the trade; to graduate her working girls into married women. She records their graduation by posting a gold star to commemorate their happy events. And there's Suzy, a girl down on her luck who takes up residence at the Bear Flag though Flora doubts she's cut out for the working life.<br /><br />Otherwise, Steinbeck happily brings back the usual suspects to the pages of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/56077.Sweet_Thursday__Cannery_Row___2_" title="Sweet Thursday (Cannery Row, #2) by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">Sweet Thursday</a>. Doc remains the "first citizen" of the row, the man to whom everyone goes for a consultation on any matter for which they need an answer. However, Doc has been to war. He returns to find his lab inactive and questioning his future, whether he has anything left to contribute in the time left to him. As usual he becomes involved in an esoteric scientific question: the topic of apoplexy in octopi. It will be the topic of a paper which he cannot seem to write. And everyone notices that Doc just doesn't seem to be himself.<br /><br />Of course, everyone on the Row wants to do something "GOOD" for Doc. However Doc is caught in a spiral of despair.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“Now discontent nibbled at him - not painfully, but constantly. Where does discontent start? You are warm enough, but you shiver. You are fed, yet hunger gnaws you. You have been loved, but your yearning wanders in new fields. And to prod all these there's time, the bastard Time. The end of life is now not so terribly far away - you can see it the way you see the finish line when you come into the stretch - and your mind says, "Have I worked enough? Have I eaten enough? Have I loved enough?" All of these, of course, are the foundation of man's greatest curse, and perhaps his greatest glory. "What has my life meant so far, and what can it mean in the time left to me?" And now we're coming to the wicked, poisoned dart: "What have I contributed in the Great Ledger? What am I worth?" And this isn't vanity or ambition. Men seem to be born with a debt they can never pay no matter how hard they try. It piles up ahead of them. Man owes something to man. If he ignores the debt it poisons him, and if he tries to make payments the debt only increases, and the quality of his gift is the measure of the man.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />In his wanderings, Doc encounters a mysterious hermit who tells Doc what his problem is. It's the lack of love. Well everyone on the Row has figured that out. Flora and Mack and the boys all think Doc needs a woman. Flora thinks Suzy is ready for a gold star.<br /><br />All the residents of the Row set out to throw Doc and Suzy together. However the two just don't seem to fit. Actually they fight like cats and dogs. But they're looking for the same thing.<br /><br />Says Doc:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“Well, I remember this girl. I am not whole without her. I am not alive without her. When she was with me I was more alive than I have ever been, and not only when she was pleasant either. Even when we were fighting I was whole.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Says Suzy:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“Maybe what I want ain't anywhere in the world, but I want it, so I think there is such a thing. I want a guy that's wide open. I want him to be a real guy, maybe even a tough guy, but I want a window in him. He can have his dukes up every other place but not with me. And he got to need the hell out of me. He got to be the kind of guy that if he aint' got me he ain't got nothing. And brother, that guy's going to have something!”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Steinbeck's return to the Row is a joyous, ribald celebration of life. It is a delight from start to finish. Minor? If you're a literary critic, maybe so. For the Steinbeck admirer, it's an absolute must. Read it.<br /><br /> <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16083166904272531427" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer854659952" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9951656863978277990">I was in Monterey quite recently and even visited the Steinbeck house in Salinas, so I thought it would be a damn good time to read another Steinbeck. As per usual, it was a really good read and as per usual, I was right. But then again, it's always a good time to read Steinbeck!<br/><br/>Having said that, I do worry every time I read one of his books, because all the character's always die and it will always be sad. The amount of hyperbole in that previous sentence is nothing to the level of my forgetful</span>
  <span id="freeText9951656863978277990" style="display:none">I was in Monterey quite recently and even visited the Steinbeck house in Salinas, so I thought it would be a damn good time to read another Steinbeck. As per usual, it was a really good read and as per usual, I was right. But then again, it's always a good time to read Steinbeck!<br /><br />Having said that, I do worry every time I read one of his books, because all the character's always die and it will always be sad. The amount of hyperbole in that previous sentence is nothing to the level of my forgetfulness when it comes to Steinbeck's solid sense of humor. For like <i>Cannery Row</i>, <i>Sweet Thursday</i> is actually a comedy.<br /><br />That makes sense, since <i>Sweet Thursday</i> is a sequel to <i>Cannery Row</i>. Most all of the old characters are back and their aims are almost exactly the same. Poor old Doc is set upon once again by Mac and the boys in their attempts do something nice for their beloved friend. Of course, they have their own happiness in mind and their ways and means aren't conducive to well-laid plans, so yes, things fall apart. That's the whole point. <br /><br />I could see someone docking the book for being a repeat and coasting on the coattails of a successful predecessor, but that someone is a douche. Shut up, sit back and enjoy the fact that a major novelist gave you more of a good thing!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9951656863978277990" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10491227209845357211">A book that is a love story on three levels. <br/><br/>We have the love that Mack, Hazel and the gang feel for Doc, their friend and mentor.   <span id="freeText10491227209845357211" style="display:none">A book that is a love story on three levels. <br /><br />We have the love that Mack, Hazel and the gang feel for Doc, their friend and mentor. They want to set him up with a home, a girl and a microscope (don't ask), and almost but not quite get it right.<br /><br />We have the slow burn love affair between Suzy and Doc. From totally different backgrounds, repelled and attracted in equal measure, but always set to get together in the end.<br /><br />Most of all we have the love that Steinbeck clearly has for Cannery Row and it's motley cast of characters. They may be living on the wrong side of town, have intermittent work prospects and have a relaxed attitude to morals and the law but their colourful and joyful lives are a treat to read. <br /><br />Written with affection and wit this is a wonderful book, worth a read.</span>

<div id="review_1053981833" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1053981833" />
    <a title="Joe Valdez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez"><img alt="Joe Valdez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585885999p2/26876584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1053981833?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Valdez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Valdez" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez">Joe Valdez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140187502" href="/book/show/816090.Sweet_Thursday">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26876584-joe-valdez?shelf=fiction-general">fiction-general</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1053981833" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10926839366052892300">Published in 1954, John Steinbeck's sequel to <i>Cannery Row</i> arrived nine years after its predecessor and directly followed the monumental world building of <i>East of Eden</i>. This might explain why <i>Sweet Thursday</i> is on much more sober footing than Steinbeck's previous literary excursions to Monterey, California; rather than loosely connected vignettes connected to a party, this is more of a fully-formed novel, with a doggone romance as its centerpiece.<br/><br/>In a prologue, Steinbeck indulges in a delightful b</span>
  <span id="freeText10926839366052892300" style="display:none">Published in 1954, John Steinbeck's sequel to <i>Cannery Row</i> arrived nine years after its predecessor and directly followed the monumental world building of <i>East of Eden</i>. This might explain why <i>Sweet Thursday</i> is on much more sober footing than Steinbeck's previous literary excursions to Monterey, California; rather than loosely connected vignettes connected to a party, this is more of a fully-formed novel, with a doggone romance as its centerpiece.<br /><br />In a prologue, Steinbeck indulges in a delightful bit of meta-fiction. Mack, king of the Palace Flophouse and leader of the indigents who squat there, comments on <i>Cannery Row</i> and how he would've written the book differently. Mack's beef is that the author should've included chapter titles to help jog the reader's memory. He then launches into general literary critique that's easy to read as an address by Steinbeck to his contemporaries:<br /><br /><i>"Well, I like a lot of talk in a book, and I don't like to have nobody tell me what the guy that's talking looks like. I want to figure out what he looks like from the way he talks. And another thing--I kind of like to figure out what the guy's thinking by what he says. I like some description too," he went on. "I like to know what color a thing is, how it smells and maybe how it looks, and maybe how a guy feels about it--but not too much of that."</i><br /><br />"Chapter 1: What Happened In Between" picks up most of the characters of <i>Cannery Row</i> after the end of World War II. Marine biologist Doc returns from a stint as a tech sergeant to find Western Biological Laboratories, which Doc had entrusted to a trust fund baby and fellow scientist known as Old Jingleballicks, run into disrepair. <br /><br />Mack and the boys (Hazel, Whitey No. 1 and Whitey No. 2) still reside rent-free at the Palace Flophouse, but Lee Chong has sold the Heavenly Flower Grocery to a street hustler from Los Angeles named Joseph and Mary, who found the odds in drug trafficking too steep and transitioned into human trafficking instead. By chance, Joseph and Mary discovered a few of his wards had musical talent. He's now found a new hustle: show business management.<br /><br />Dora Flood, owner of the Bear Flag Restaurant, died in her sleep. The whorehouse has been inherited by her next of kin, an older sister from San Francisco named Flora, who's rechristened "Fauna" by one of the regulars. Fauna runs the Bear Flag as a finishing school of sorts, priding herself in marrying her girls off to proper gentlemen. Against every instinct, Fauna takes in a new arrival to Monterey named Suzy, a tough kid whose heart (as well as her mouth) is too big to make it as a hustler. <br /><br />With no wife and no children, Doc has begun to think seriously about his legacy and hits upon the idea to write a paper on cephalopods, noting his many detailed observations about the moods of octopi. Instead, he develops a bad case of writer's block and a cloud falls over all of Cannery Row. Fauna sees that Suzy is too opinionated to excel as an employee of the Bear Flag and needs a husband as much as Mack needs a wife to lift him out of his malaise. <br /><br />Meanwhile, Mack and the boys are worried that the deed to the Palace Flophouse has been sold to Joseph and Mary without his knowledge but when the new owner receives a tax bill, the tenants will be expected to pay rent. Mack's brainstorm is to raffle off the "deed" to the flophouse, using the raffle to buy Doc an expensive microscope and rig the contest so that he inherits the flophouse as well. Fauna and Mack's schemes converge, without Doc or Suzy having much to say about it. <br /><br /><i>Sweet Thursday</i> (the day between Lousy Wednesday and Waiting Friday) is a delight. Steinbeck conjures a world I didn't want to leave. The climate and atmosphere of postwar Monterey -- with its shuttered canneries, its fishing fleets, its working girls, philosophers and charlatans -- is just one facet. Steinbeck is a student of human nature and has a wonderful way of noting what's wrong with people without being bitten by bitterness or cynicism.<br /><br />Several of the characters of Cannery Row get the idea to do something nice for Doc, when in the end, they do what they really want, which is something nice for the person they know best: themselves. But their scams are always out in the open. Characters lie, cheat, steal, slack off and take advantage of each other, but they feel bad about it too. And with Monterey as small as it is, they have to live with their guilt and atone for it in some way, which I found hopeful. This usually leads to a greater disaster, but it makes for delightful reading.<br /><br />Somewhere in there is the story of the whole human race.<br /><br />I leave you with some wisdom from Steinbeck regarding choosing a wife.<br /><br /><i>"You know, they say there's three good reasons for marrying a hustler."<br /><br />"What are you talking about?" said Doc.<br /><br />Mack counted on his fingers. "Number one, she ain't likely to wander--she's done all her experimenting. Number two, you ain't likely to surprise her or disappoint her. And number three, if a hustler goes for you she ain't got but one reason."<br /><br />Doc watched him, hypnotized. "What reason?"<br /><br />"She likes you. Good night, Doc."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="10926839366052892300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_1053981833'><a id="like_count_review_1053981833" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1053981833?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">25 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F56077.Sweet_Thursday">Like</a></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_284059860" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/284059860" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4542979-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454402751p2/4542979.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/284059860?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/4542979-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4542979-kim?shelf=all-time-favourites">all-time-favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4542979-kim?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer284059860" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11632834325446166272"><br/>Since listening to an audiobook edition of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4799.Cannery_Row' title='Cannery Row by John Steinbeck' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cannery Row</a> earlier this year and falling in love with both the characters and Steinbeck's writing, I've looked forward to reading this sequel. It's set about ten years after the events of <i>Cannery Row</i>. Doc has returned from army service to his work at the Western Biological Laboratory and finds himself unsatisfied and depressed. This makes the other denizens of Cannery Row decide that Doc needs a wife and that Suzy - a new girl at the Bear Flag Restaura</span>
  <span id="freeText11632834325446166272" style="display:none"><br />Since listening to an audiobook edition of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4799.Cannery_Row" title="Cannery Row by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">Cannery Row</a> earlier this year and falling in love with both the characters and Steinbeck's writing, I've looked forward to reading this sequel. It's set about ten years after the events of <i>Cannery Row</i>. Doc has returned from army service to his work at the Western Biological Laboratory and finds himself unsatisfied and depressed. This makes the other denizens of Cannery Row decide that Doc needs a wife and that Suzy - a new girl at the Bear Flag Restaurant - is the wife for him. <br /><br />Unlike <i>Cannery Row</i>, which is essentially a series of linked vignettes, <i>Sweet Thursday</i> has a more traditional structure. In many ways it resembles a 1950s romantic comedy - think Spencer Tracey and Katharine Hepburn* - in which boy meets girl, boy loses girl, boy gets girl. In the process of finding true love, both Doc and Suzy have to learn a lot about themselves and about each other. Getting Doc and Suzy together requires effort from Doc's friends, including Mack and the boys at the Palace Flophouse (and in particular the dimwitted but sweet Hazel), Dora and the girls at the Bear Flag Restaurant and the new owner of Lee Chong's Grocery, who revels in the name of Jesus and Mary.<br /><br />I loved reading this novel. The characters are well-drawn and the narrative contains both humour and wisdom about the human condition. I love Steinbeck's use of language: there's something about the directness of his prose, its accessibility and its beauty, which really speaks to me. This is not a high-brow work. It may not be up there with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4395.The_Grapes_of_Wrath" title="The Grapes of Wrath by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">The Grapes of Wrath</a> in terms of literary merit, but for anyone who has read and loved <i>Cannery Row</i>, it's a real treat. I will definitely be reading it again. Spending time with Doc and those who love him is a joy. <br /><br />*Not that I visualise Tracey and Hepburn in the roles of Doc and Suzy, but the sparring and the repartee which characterised their on-screen relationship would be just right for this pair.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11632834325446166272" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review284059860">

<div id="review_2310678" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2310678" />
    <a title="Meghan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83627-meghan"><img alt="Meghan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1181001895p2/83627.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2310678?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meghan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meghan" href="/user/show/83627-meghan">Meghan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140187502" href="/book/show/816090.Sweet_Thursday">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who liked Cannery Row</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2310678" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3740647845827848221">The sequel to Steinbeck's <i>Cannery Row</i>, it's difficult to nail down exactly why this novel isn't as good as its prequel. <i>Sweet Thursday</i> is still a very enjoyable book and managed to arouse and audible chuckle from me here and there, but, as the only work of Steinbeck's I've read that could be labeled as a "romantic comedy," it fell short of leaving me with that happy and slightly amazed feeling that <i>Cannery Row</i> succeeded in leaving behind. There were points in the story where I almost wanted to g</span>
  <span id="freeText3740647845827848221" style="display:none">The sequel to Steinbeck's <i>Cannery Row</i>, it's difficult to nail down exactly why this novel isn't as good as its prequel. <i>Sweet Thursday</i> is still a very enjoyable book and managed to arouse and audible chuckle from me here and there, but, as the only work of Steinbeck's I've read that could be labeled as a "romantic comedy," it fell short of leaving me with that happy and slightly amazed feeling that <i>Cannery Row</i> succeeded in leaving behind. There were points in the story where I almost wanted to groan and say, "Really, Steinbeck? Really?"<br /><br />Don't get me wrong. This is still a good, fun book. Most of the lovable characters from <i>Cannery Row</i> return for new adventures and most of these characters have not lost their endearing and poetic starkness, but it's just not the same. There's something implicitly philosophical about a plot that consists basically of a hodgepodge of bums trying to throw a party for their beloved "Doc." When those same bums--give and take a few--take it upon themselves to find a <i>wife</i>--or something like a wife--for Doc, I just don't buy it. It's somehow not as meaningful. <br /><br />But that's okay. If you liked <i>Cannery Row</i> then you should still read <i>Sweet Thursday</i>. Who knows? You might completely disagree with me and like it just as much. It's entirely possible.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3740647845827848221" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/8275434" />
    <a title="Miriam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/150249-miriam"><img alt="Miriam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1183218481p2/150249.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/8275434?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Miriam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Miriam" href="/user/show/150249-miriam">Miriam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8275434" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5968117111896049455">The second time this book has caught me by surprise. He's got a very... I wanna say "astute," but it's something else. He just keeps "gettin'" (like GOTCHA!) me. Steinbeck has a voice I can tune in to. It's weird, like how singing voices resonate whether I like the music or not, styles of writing... they either rattle your bones, or it's nothing. Just a story. Words completely randomly and inelegantly strung together. end rant. a-a-a-and... :) here's the part...<br/><br/>----------------------<br/><br/>"... "You kn</span>
  <span id="freeText5968117111896049455" style="display:none">The second time this book has caught me by surprise. He's got a very... I wanna say "astute," but it's something else. He just keeps "gettin'" (like GOTCHA!) me. Steinbeck has a voice I can tune in to. It's weird, like how singing voices resonate whether I like the music or not, styles of writing... they either rattle your bones, or it's nothing. Just a story. Words completely randomly and inelegantly strung together. end rant. a-a-a-and... :) here's the part...<br /><br />----------------------<br /><br />"... "You know, Suzy, there ain't no way in the world to get in trouble by keeping your mouth shut. You look back at every mess you ever got in and you'll find your tongue started it."<br /><br />"That's true," said Suzy. "But I can't seem to stop."<br /><br />"You got to learn it like you learn anything else---just practice. The next thing is opinions. Hell Suzy, we ain't got no opinions! We just say stuff we heard or seen in the movies. We're scared we'll miss something, like running for a bus. That's the second rule: lay off opinions because you ain't really got any."<br /><br />"You got 'em numbered, huh?" said Suzy.<br /><br />"I could write a book," said Fauna. "'If She Could, I Could.' Now take number three. There don't nobody listen, and it's so easy! You don't have to do nothing when you listen. If you do listen it's pretty interesting. If a guy says something that pricks up your interest, why, don't hide it from him, kind of try to wonder what he's thinking instead of how you're going to answer him back."<br /><br />"You're sure putting the finger on me," Suzy said softly.<br /><br />"I only got a little more, but it's the hardest of all, and the easiest."<br /><br />"What number?"<br /><br />"I lost track. Don't pretend to be something you ain't, and don't make like you know something you don't, or sooner or later you'll sure fall on your ass. And there's one more part to this one, whatever number it is: they ain't nobody was ever insulted by a question. S'pose Doc says something and you don't know what it means. Ask him! The nicest thing in the world you can do for anybody is let them help you.""<br /><br />----------------<br /><br />I don't know. That's just a piece. I just keep getting surprised by moments he hosts, and it's in good context! not just thrown in there!<br /><br />If you like Monterey or simple times of... some time ago :) I like his style. It's simple, but lovely. I feel like I think it's boring subject matter, it's the minutiae of life, but it's strung together so nice and so thoughtfully it's like looking at a beautifully painted picture of an orange. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5968117111896049455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Whenever someone tells me they enjoyed Cannery Row, I say: read Sweet Thursday; it's better. Steinbeck invented such wonderful characters and breathed life into them. </span>

          </span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5572803301374166258">I loved Cannery Row and it was a love that developed over many years of growing appreciation. Sweet Thursday had everything I loved about Cannery Row (it being a sequel and all). I can't get over how good Steinbeck is at characterization; he shows, not tells. There were some slow parts and bizarre parts, much like Cannery Row but I really enjoyed it and bought into everything that happened. <br/><br/>You got to watch Hazel though. That boy is trouble.<br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1443894267" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1443894267" />

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1443894267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10668535970962338601">Having enjoyed Cannery Row so much, it was with some trepidation that I began this one - I'm not a big fan of sequels and think that they are often poorly thought out, ill conceived or just plain lazy. However, this one proved to be an exception and is well worth a read - it definitely added to the first book rather than detracted which is often the case. Any fan of Steinbeck should definitely make the effort to get hold of this one!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_741048833" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/741048833" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/741048833?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780142429242" href="/book/show/10827272-sweet-thursday">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=downpour">downpour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=love">love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=usa">usa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer741048833" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14256519077034106925">I had three hours left of this nine hour audiobook, and I was <u>still</u> not enjoying it. But I persisted. This is one of those books that it takes a while to warm to. By the end I cared for some of the characters (not just Doc, but Fauna and Hazel too) and there was some humor that made me smile. It has a "cute" ending. How do you rate a book that for the most part bores you but then at the end it turns for the better? By the end I liked it, but not in the beginning and not in the middle either! It </span>
  <span id="freeText14256519077034106925" style="display:none">I had three hours left of this nine hour audiobook, and I was <u>still</u> not enjoying it. But I persisted. This is one of those books that it takes a while to warm to. By the end I cared for some of the characters (not just Doc, but Fauna and Hazel too) and there was some humor that made me smile. It has a "cute" ending. How do you rate a book that for the most part bores you but then at the end it turns for the better? By the end I liked it, but not in the beginning and not in the middle either! It is too much a repeat of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4799.Cannery_Row" title="Cannery Row by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">Cannery Row</a>.<br /><br />********************************************<br /><br />When I had listened to 2/3 of the book I was annoyed, so annoyed that I had to stop and write a review just to let off steam. I am thinking you should hear both points of view. Here is what I wrote then: <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/56077.Sweet_Thursday" title="Sweet Thursday by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">Sweet Thursday</a> is the second in Steinbeck’s series following <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4799.Cannery_Row" title="Cannery Row by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">Cannery Row</a> (my review: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/551071241">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> ). However this second book is just sort of a repeat! In this book I feel no empathy for any of the characters. In this book they all feel like a bunch of misfits. In this book I don't even love Doc. Will there be a big party at the end? Authors rarely can produce a series where the momentum is sustained. This is boring, and the humor does NOT make me laugh. <br /><br />I LOVED <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4395.The_Grapes_of_Wrath" title="The Grapes of Wrath by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">The Grapes of Wrath</a> ( <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/547635545">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> ). THAT is a masterpiece! I was clearly disappointed by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4406.East_of_Eden" title="East of Eden by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">East of Eden</a>; there I felt like the message was being hammered into me ( <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/554604060">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> ). <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5306.Travels_with_Charley_In_Search_of_America" title="Travels with Charley In Search of America by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">Travels with Charley: In Search of America</a> was funny and sad and insightful, all rolled into a thin little book (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/388998565">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> ).<br /><br />For me, <u>Sweet Thursday</u> is a repeat and terribly b-o-r-i-n-g! <br /> <br /><br />I listened to the audiobook narrated by Jerry Farden. A good narrator can unfortunately not recreate a boring book.........<br /></span>

<div id="review_563782498" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/563782498" />
    <a title="Jamie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51089-jamie"><img alt="Jamie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1286573170p2/51089.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/563782498?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/51089-jamie">Jamie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51089-jamie?shelf=the-classics">the-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51089-jamie?shelf=the-wild-west">the-wild-west</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer563782498" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6564954268624939855">Had I known this was a sequel to <i><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/333687846' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cannery Row</a>,</i> I would have read this the minute I finished <i>Cannery Row</i>. (How did I not know there was a sequel to <i>Cannery Row</i>!) But, thank you, Elmore Leonard. I decided that this year, I’m taking my favorite authors and reading the books they’ve read. To follow that thread, that slow-burn rapture, the world unfolding from a singular point of view. And thus: I found <i>Sweet Thursday</i>, the sequel to <i>Cannery Row</i>, from the man that makes more sense than anybody that he’</span>
  <span id="freeText6564954268624939855" style="display:none">Had I known this was a sequel to <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/333687846" rel="nofollow noopener">Cannery Row</a>,</i> I would have read this the minute I finished <i>Cannery Row</i>. (How did I not know there was a sequel to <i>Cannery Row</i>!) But, thank you, Elmore Leonard. I decided that this year, I’m taking my favorite authors and reading the books they’ve read. To follow that thread, that slow-burn rapture, the world unfolding from a singular point of view. And thus: I found <i>Sweet Thursday</i>, the sequel to <i>Cannery Row</i>, from the man that makes more sense than anybody that he’s read <i>Sweet Thursday</i>, the sequel to <i>Cannery Row</i>.<br /><br />There’s a line in <i>Cannery Row</i> I want to use every time I talk about one of Elmore’s books. It goes: “Its inhabitants are, as the man once said, ‘whores, pimps, gamblers, and sons of bitches,’ by which he meant Everybody. Had the man looked through another peephole he might have said, ‘Saints and angels and martyrs and holy men,’ and he would have meant the same thing.” <br /><br />And <i>Sweet Thursday</i>— it’s <i>Cannery Row</i> squared, just with all the more joy. It’s hilarious. It’s mad and brilliant. It’s a crackpot little satire with all the love in the world. I mean, find me a book these days where that doesn’t apply? It’s one I don’t have much interest in reading. Give me the sons of bitches and martyrs and holy men; a whorehouse and a flophouse, let me call it ‘home, sweet home’ and mean the same thing. <blockquote>“She’s making a patsy of you,” said the Patrón. <br />“People got to be a patsy now and then,” said Fauna. “You never feel real good if you never been a sucker.”</blockquote></span>

  <span id="freeText17525833145891997752" style="display:none">I think I read this book eons ago in my early 20s, but it wasn’t that familiar to me so it’s possible I only intended to or I only read Cannery Row. <br />This book has an element of the fantastic to it, but that doesn’t take away from its comedy, sweetness and charm. There were laugh out loud moments in the story and it was a pleasure to read. <br />Many of the crew from Cannery Row returned after the war minus a few who were either died, were killed in the war, or moved away. The market was sold by Lee Chong to Joseph and Mary Revas who is a hustler who recently moved into the Row. Mack remained behind to keep an eye on everything as the canneries had all shut down from over fishing of sardines. There’s even a new madam at the Bear Flag Brothel. Add Doc and a new woman who just got off the bus looking for work and you’ve got a delightful novel. I will read this again someday.</span>

<div id="review_381717764" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/381717764" />
    <a title="Melissa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9255064-melissa"><img alt="Melissa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544028570p2/9255064.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/381717764?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa" href="/user/show/9255064-melissa">Melissa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer381717764" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5231181489647316218">Sweet Thursday is the sequel to Cannery Row, one of my favorite of Steinbeck’s books. I’ve read the epic masterpieces, like East of Eden and Grapes of Wrath. I’ve read the shorter morality tales, like Of Mice and Men and The Pearl. Yet after all of those brilliant works, my favorites remain his road trip memoir, Travels with Charley, and Cannery Row. I may get more depth and inner turmoil from his other works, but these are the ones I relate, the ones I want to return to. <br/><br/>Sweet Thursday quickly </span>
  <span id="freeText5231181489647316218" style="display:none">Sweet Thursday is the sequel to Cannery Row, one of my favorite of Steinbeck’s books. I’ve read the epic masterpieces, like East of Eden and Grapes of Wrath. I’ve read the shorter morality tales, like Of Mice and Men and The Pearl. Yet after all of those brilliant works, my favorites remain his road trip memoir, Travels with Charley, and Cannery Row. I may get more depth and inner turmoil from his other works, but these are the ones I relate, the ones I want to return to. <br /><br />Sweet Thursday quickly made its way to that top bracket as I read it. Steinbeck takes us back to Cannery Row and all of our favorite characters. We return shortly after the end of World War II and learn that Doc has been away, serving his country and Mack has been holding down the fort. The grocer, Lee Chong, is long gone and there are some new characters in the town. <br /><br />Steinbeck gives us some of my favorite literary characters in this book. Doc, Mack and the others won a place in our hearts in Cannery Row, but the new additions are just as wonderful. There’s a selfish con artist named Old Jingleballicks and a Mexican man who runs the grocery store named Joseph and Mary (often referred to as J and M). Suzy, a young woman looking for guidance, provides a unique spark to the story. Her transformation throughout the book is one of the most rewarding I’ve read, because you can’t help but root for her. <br /><br />**SPOILERS**<br /><br />I loved that Steinbeck didn’t throw Doc and Suzy together immediately. It felt so right that Suzy had a chance to get her life together before ending up with him. She needed to find her own balance and believe in herself before committing to another person. Once she had a room of her own, she finally had pride in herself and once she had that, she had something to offer someone else. <br /><br />**SPOILERS OVER**<br /><br />Of course, you can’t forget Hazel, one of Steinbeck’s greatest creations. He’s naïve and sweet, and devoted to his friends. When he’s in trouble or being taken advantage of, his friends step up and we see the best in everyone around him as they protect their friend. Hazel becomes a key player in this novel. He’s put to the test as he tries to work out the best way to help Doc, the man he admires so much. <br /><br />One of my favorite chapters is called “One Night of Love,” which chronicles Suzy and Doc’s first date. The two couldn’t be more different, but there’s an unexpected sweetness that we witness when they both let their guards down. That private moment is beautiful.<br /><br />Sweet Thursday was everything I hoped it would be. It’s touching, funny and profound in an unexplainably simple way. It makes you wish you lived on Cannery Row and could share a beer with Doc and his friends. It reminds you of the goodness the lies within every person and the fact that sometimes you just need the right situation to bring it out. <br /><br />“I love true things,” said Doc. “Even when they hurt. Isn’t it better to know the truth about oneself?” <br /><br />“S-l-o-w-ness it gave meaning to everything. It made everything royal.” <br /><br />“No one knows how greatness comes to a man. It may lie in his blackness, sleeping, or it may lance into him like those driven fiery particles from outer space. These things, however, are known about greatness: need gives it life and puts it in action; it never comes without pain; it leaves a man changed, chastened, and exalted at the same time – he can never return to simplicity.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="5231181489647316218" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_462976316" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/462976316" />
    <a title="Primero Fin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14886570-primero-fin"><img alt="Primero Fin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353824346p2/14886570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/462976316?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Primero Fin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Primero Fin" href="/user/show/14886570-primero-fin">Primero Fin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer462976316" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8711858368478179416">It is impossible for me to separate Sweet Thursday from Cannery Row - so I cannot objectively comment on Sweet Thursday as a standalone novel. <br/><br/>Cannery Row is one of my favorite novels. Beginning as a high school assignment and continuing over the next 40 years I have read it a dozen times. Someone told me that Steinbeck wrote Cannery Row as a gift to the country during WWII - as something to make people happy. He succeeded in that goal. Cannery Row is simply magical. It has that certain 'somethi</span>
  <span id="freeText8711858368478179416" style="display:none">It is impossible for me to separate Sweet Thursday from Cannery Row - so I cannot objectively comment on Sweet Thursday as a standalone novel. <br /><br />Cannery Row is one of my favorite novels. Beginning as a high school assignment and continuing over the next 40 years I have read it a dozen times. Someone told me that Steinbeck wrote Cannery Row as a gift to the country during WWII - as something to make people happy. He succeeded in that goal. Cannery Row is simply magical. It has that certain 'something' that all masterpieces have. I won't try to define it or describe it.<br /><br />Years after reading Cannery Row (and nearly everything else that Steinbeck wrote) I was stunned to find Sweet Thursday - a sequel to Cannery Row. How could I not know about this? <br /><br />Boy was I disappointed. Sweet Thursday has the feel of a typical Hollywood money-grab sequel. The setting is the same. Some of the characters are back. But there's no magic. Nada. Reading Sweet Thursday reminded me of re-uniting with a long-lost friend and finding the magic gone, the fire's out, you can't go home again.<br /><br />I love almost everything that Steinbeck wrote - Sweet Thursday, along with Winter of Our Discontent, is on the very short list of Steinbeck's work that left me cold. Looking at the chronology of Steinbeck's novels I now realize that Sweet Thursday marked the beginning Steinbeck's decline. It is shocking how fast he lost his touch. But I'm grateful for how long he had the magic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8711858368478179416" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141185521" href="/book/show/609516.Sweet_Thursday">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51723834" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12629951214187887219">I had read Cannery Row something like 15 years ago, and had forgotten a lot of the characters. So, it may not be essential to have read it prior to reading Sweet Thursday, but it does help. <br/><br/>There is a kind of style or attitude that Steinbeck brings to this world which is both sympathetic and very humorous. That's a difficult trick as it would be very easy for us to laugh at Hazel - a man who believes he will become President because of a horoscope reading from the madame of the local whore house</span>
  <span id="freeText12629951214187887219" style="display:none">I had read Cannery Row something like 15 years ago, and had forgotten a lot of the characters. So, it may not be essential to have read it prior to reading Sweet Thursday, but it does help. <br /><br />There is a kind of style or attitude that Steinbeck brings to this world which is both sympathetic and very humorous. That's a difficult trick as it would be very easy for us to laugh at Hazel - a man who believes he will become President because of a horoscope reading from the madame of the local whore house, even though he has no aspiration or ability towards the Presidency. Surface descriptions of most characters could imply a lighthearted derision. Steinbeck avoids this kind of derisive humor as well as sentimentality and produces a comedic novel that is both funny and meaningful. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12629951214187887219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=literature">literature</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer11835644334694017108"><blockquote><i>"There are people who will say that this whole account is a lie, but a thing isn't necessarily a lie even if it didn't necessarily happen."</i> (54)</blockquote>I loved <i>Cannery Row</i>, and thought I'd finally read <i>Sweet Thursday</i>, which is set in the same place and returns—after the war—to some of the same characters. I didn't think it was as good as the first sketch of the Row, but the swashbuckling group created by Steinbeck, plus/minus a couple of souls, was still entertaining and endearing. </span>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>
